{"nick":"joepie91","message":"Sleepy_Coder: so the idea of propagated capabilities is, summarized, that you can grant a particular chunk of code access to a protected resource (a particular part of the filesystem, more CPU power, whatever), and that chunk of code can propagate any of the capabilities it receives to anything that *it* calls/instantiates/loads, but only those that it got itself","date":"2018-07-16T09:54:21.464Z","type":"message"}

{"nick":"joepie91","message":"tamarind: because there are two different arrow syntaxes; one of them, `(foo) => thing` expects `thing` to be an expression, whereas `(foo) => { thing }` expects `thing` to be a statement","date":"2018-07-16T12:12:06.306Z","type":"message"}
{"nick":"joepie91","message":"a `return foo` is a statement that tells a function to return whatever `foo` is (which needs to be an expression)","date":"2018-07-16T12:12:28.449Z","type":"message"}
{"nick":"joepie91","message":"so `(foo) => thing` is equivalent to `(foo) => { return thing; }`","date":"2018-07-16T12:12:39.852Z","type":"message"}
{"nick":"joepie91","message":"however, I recommend avoiding the brace-less syntax unless you have a very good reason for it, because in most cases it will harm readability","date":"2018-07-16T12:12:57.845Z","type":"message"}
{"nick":"tamarind","message":"ah ! ok thank you very much","date":"2018-07-16T12:13:20.725Z","type":"message"}

{"nick":"joepie91","message":"where the brace-less variant clearly expresses your intention","date":"2018-07-16T12:13:55.229Z","type":"message"}
